    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The chromosome-level quality genome provides insights into the evolution of the biosynthesis genes for aroma compounds of Osmanthus fragrans"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2018 5:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-11-20"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Sweet osmanthus (Osmanthus fragrans) is a very popular ornamental tree species throughout Southeast Asia and USA particularly for its extremely fragrant aroma. We constructed a chromosome-level reference genome of O. fragrans to assist in studies of the evolution, genetic diversity, and molecular mechanism of aroma development. A total of over 118&#8201;Gb of polished reads was produced from HiSeq (45.1&#8201;Gb) and PacBio Sequel (73.35&#8201;Gb), giving 100&#215; depth coverage for long reads. The combination of Illumina-short reads, PacBio-long reads, and Hi-C data produced the final chromosome quality genome of O. fragrans with a genome size of 727&#8201;Mb and a heterozygosity of 1.45 %. The genome was annotated using de novo and homology comparison and further refined with transcriptome data. The genome of O. fragrans was predicted to have&#8201;45,542 genes, of which 95.68&#160;% were functionally annotated. Genome annotation found 49.35&#160;% as the repetitive sequences, with long terminal repeats (LTR) being the richest (28.94&#160;%). Genome evolution analysis indicated the evidence of whole-genome duplication 15 million years ago, which contributed to the current content of 45,242 genes. Metabolic analysis revealed that linalool, a monoterpene is the main aroma compound. Based on the genome and transcriptome, we further demonstrated the direct connection between terpene synthases (TPSs) and the rich aromatic molecules in O. fragrans. We identified three new flower-specific TPS genes, of which the expression coincided with the production of linalool. Our results suggest that the high number of TPS genes and the flower tissue- and stage-specific TPS genes expressions might drive the strong unique aroma production of O. fragrans. A high-quality genome sequence for sweet olive, Osmanthus fragrans, has revealed which genes make the ornamental tree&#8217;s blossoms so fragrant. Lianggui Wang at Nanjing Forestry University in China and coworkers used cutting-edge approaches to produce a high-resolution picture of sweet olive&#8217;s genome. Analysis of the full sequence revealed that sweet olive has a large number of genes, more than 45,000. Further investigation showed that the entire genome had been duplicated, or accidentally copied, several million years ago. Thanks to the duplication, sweet olive has extra copies of the genes that produce its scent compound, resulting in its very strong scent. These results are only the beginning. Having this first sequence makes sequencing other varieties of sweet olive much easier, and future comparisons between genomes will help to pinpoint genes that control many other traits."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Sweet osmanthus (Osmanthus fragrans) is a very popular ornamental tree species throughout Southeast Asia and USA particularly for its extremely fragrant aroma. Metabolic analysis revealed that linalool, a monoterpene is the main aroma compound. A high-quality genome sequence for sweet olive, Osmanthus fragrans, has revealed which genes make the ornamental tree&#8217;s blossoms so fragrant. Further investigation showed that the entire genome had been duplicated, or accidentally copied, several million years ago. Thanks to the duplication, sweet olive has extra copies of the genes that produce its scent compound, resulting in its very strong scent. These results are only the beginning. Having this first sequence makes sequencing other varieties of sweet olive much easier, and future comparisons between genomes will help to pinpoint genes that control many other traits."/>

        <meta property="og:title" content="The chromosome-level quality genome provides insights into the evolution of the biosynthesis genes for aroma compounds of Osmanthus fragrans"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="A high-quality genome sequence for sweet olive, Osmanthus fragrans, has revealed which genes make the ornamental treeâs blossoms so fragrant. Lianggui Wang at Nanjing Forestry University in China and coworkers used cutting-edge approaches to produce a high-resolution picture of sweet oliveâs genome. Analysis of the full sequence revealed that sweet olive has a large number of genes, more than 45,000. Further investigation showed that the entire genome had been duplicated, or accidentally copied, several million years ago. Thanks to the duplication, sweet olive has extra copies of the genes that produce its scent compound, resulting in its very strong scent. These results are only the beginning. Having this first sequence makes sequencing other varieties of sweet olive much easier, and future comparisons between genomes will help to pinpoint genes that control many other traits."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/m685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41438_2018_108_Fig1_HTML.png"/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Sweet osmanthus (<i>Osmanthus fragrans</i>) is a very popular ornamental tree species throughout Southeast Asia and USA particularly for its extremely fragrant aroma. We constructed a chromosome-level reference genome of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> to assist in studies of the evolution, genetic diversity, and molecular mechanism of aroma development. A total of over 118âGb of polished reads was produced from HiSeq (45.1âGb) and PacBio Sequel (73.35âGb), giving 100Ã depth coverage for long reads. The combination of Illumina-short reads, PacBio-long reads, and Hi-C data produced the final chromosome quality genome of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> with a genome size of 727âMb and a heterozygosity of 1.45 %. The genome was annotated using de novo and homology comparison and further refined with transcriptome data. The genome of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> was predicted to haveâ45,542 genes, of which 95.68Â % were functionally annotated. Genome annotation found 49.35Â % as the repetitive sequences, with long terminal repeats (LTR) being the richest (28.94Â %). Genome evolution analysis indicated the evidence of whole-genome duplication 15 million years ago, which contributed to the current content of 45,242 genes. Metabolic analysis revealed that linalool, a monoterpene is the main aroma compound. Based on the genome and transcriptome, we further demonstrated the direct connection between terpene synthases (TPSs) and the rich aromatic molecules in <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i>. We identified three new flower-specific <i>TPS</i> genes, of which the expression coincided with the production of linalool. Our results suggest that the high number of <i>TPS</i> genes and the flower tissue- and stage-specific <i>TPS</i> genes expressions might drive the strong unique aroma production of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i>.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Sweet osmanthus (Dicotyledons, Lamiales, Oleaceae, <i>Osmanthus</i>) is one of the most popular, evergreen ornamental tree species in China due to its unique sweet aroma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Shang, F. D., Yin, Y. J. &amp; Xiang, Q. B. The culture of sweet osmanthus in China. J. Henan Univ. Nat. Sci. 43, 136â139 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d50438e605">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hao, R. M., Zang, D. K. &amp; Xiang, Q. B. Investigation on natural resources of Osmanthus fragrans Lour. at Zhou luo cun in Hunan. Acta Hortic. Sin. 32, 926â929 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d50438e608">2</a></sup>. More than 160 cultivars of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> have been classified based on phenotypes such as the leaf shape, flower color, aroma, season and frequency of flower blooming<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Zang, D. K., Xiang, Q. B., Liu, Y. L. &amp; Hao, R. M. The studying history and the application to International Cultivar Registration Authority of sweet osmanthus (Osmanthus fragrans Lour.). J. Plant Resour. Environ. 12, 49â53 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d50438e618">3</a></sup>. The association between phenotypes and genotypes of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> has been examined through aroma compounds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Deng, C. H., Song, G. X. &amp; Hu, Y. M. Application of HS-SPME and GC-MS to characterization of volatile compounds emitted from Osmanthus flowers. Ann. Chim. 94, 921â927 (2004)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d50438e629">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xin, H. P. et al. Characterization of volatile compounds in flowers from four groups of sweet osmanthus (Osmanthus fragrans) cultivars. Can. J. Plant Sci. 93, 923â931 (2013)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d50438e629_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Cai, X. et al. &amp; W, C.Y. Analysis of aroma-active compounds in three sweet osmanthus (Osmanthus fragrans) cultivars by gas-chromatography olfactometry and GC-mass spectrometry. J. Zhejiang. Univ. Sci. B 15, 638â648 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d50438e632">6</a></sup>, essential oils<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hu, C. D. et al. Essential oil composition of Osmanthus fragrans varieties by GC-MS and heuristic evolving latent projections. Oil Plants 15, 832â838 (2012)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d50438e636_2">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Lei, G. M. et al. Water-soluble essential oil components of fresh flowers of Osmanthus fragrans lour. J. Essent. Oil Res. 28, 177â184 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d50438e639">10</a></sup>, and taxonomy using various molecular markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Shang, F. Sin. 31, 685â687 (2004)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d50438e643">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yuan, W. J., Han, Y. J., Dong, M. F. &amp; Shang, F. D. Assessment of genetic diversity and relationships among Osmanthus fragrans cultivars using AFLP markers. Electron. J. Biotechnol. 14, 2â3 (2011)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d50438e643_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hu, W., Luo, Y., Yang, Y., Zhang, Z. Y. &amp; Fan, D. M. Genetic diversity and population genetic structure of wild sweet osmanthus revealed by microsatellite markers. Acta Hortic. Sin. 41, 1427â1435 (2014)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d50438e643_2">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yuan, W. J., Li, Y., Ma, Y. F., Han, Y. J. &amp; Shang, F. D. Isolation and characterization of microsatellite markers for Osmanthus fragrans (Oleaceae) using 454 sequencing technology. Genet. Mol. Res. 14, 17154â17158 (2015)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d50438e643_3">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Han, Y. J. et al. cDNA-AFLP analysis on 2 Osmanthus fragrans cultivars with different flower color and molecular characteristics of MYB1gene. Trees 29, 931â940 (2015)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d50438e643_4">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="He, Y. X., Yuan, W. J., Dong, M. F., Han, Y. J. &amp; Shang, F. D. The first genetic map in sweet osmanthus (Osmanthus fragrans Lour.) using specific locus amplified fragment sequencing. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 1621 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d50438e646">16</a></sup>. Transcriptome studies have determined the genes that might be responsible for the emission of flower scent in <i>O. fragrans</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zhang, X. S., Pei, J. J., Zhao, L. G., Tang, F. &amp; Fang, X. Y. RNA-Seq analysis and comparison of the enzymes involved in ionone synthesis of three cultivars of Osmanthus. J. Asian Nat. Prod. Res. 9, 1â13 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d50438e653">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yang, X. L. et al. Transcriptomic analysis of the candidate genes related to aroma formation in Osmanthus fragrans. Molecules 23, 1604 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d50438e656">18</a></sup>. Gene expression has also been modulated at different flowering stages of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Xu, C. et al. Cloning and expression analysis of MEP pathway enzyme-encoding genes in Osmanthus fragrans. Genes 7, 78 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d50438e665">19</a></sup>. Differential gene expression studies have identified genes in the mediated isopentenol production (MEP) pathway, as well as the terpenoid- and carotenoid-synthesis pathways. Transcriptomics studies allowed researchers to make connections between the major flower aroma compounds, and differentially expressed genes and encoded proteins. The flower aroma compounds, (<i>R</i>)- and (<i>S</i>)-linanool are produced by a terpene synthase(s) (TPS)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zeng, X. L. et al. Emission and accumulation of monoterpene and the key terpene synthase (TPS) associated with monoterpene biosynthesis in Osmanthus fragrans Lour. Front. Plant Sci. 6, 1232 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d50438e676">20</a></sup>. Another key flower aroma compound, <i>Î²</i>-ionone, is produced through the oxidative cleavage of <i>Î²</i>-carotene by carotenoid cleavage enzymes (CCD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Baldermann, S. et al. Functional characterization of a carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase 1 and its relation to the carotenoid accumulation and volatile emission during the floral development of Osmanthus fragrans Lour. J. Exp. Bot. 61, 2967â2977 (2010)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d50438e686">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Baldermann, S., Kato, M., Fleischmann, P. &amp; Watanabe, N. Biosynthesis of Î±- and Î²-ionone, prominent scent compounds, in flowers of Osmanthus fragrans. Acta Biochim. Pol. 59, 79â81 (2012)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d50438e686_1">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Han, Y. J., Liu, L. X., Dong, M. F., Yuan, W. J. &amp; Shang, F. D. cDNA cloning of the phytoene synthase (PSY) and expression analysis of PSY and carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase genes in Osmanthus fragrans. Biologia 68, 258â263 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d50438e689">23</a></sup>. Transcriptome studies have shown that TPS(s) and CCD(s) are differentially expressed at different flowering stages in <i>O. fragrans</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Han, Y. J. et al. Differential expression of carotenoid-related genes determines diversified carotenoid coloration in flower petal of Osmanthus fragrans. Tree. Genet. Genom. 10, 329â338 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d50438e695">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhang, C., Wang, Y. G., Fu, J. X., Bao, Z. Y. &amp; Zhao, H. B. Transcriptomic analysis and carotenogenic gene expression related to petal coloration in Osmanthus fragrans âYanhong Guiâ. Trees 30, 1207â1223 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d50438e698">25</a></sup>. Additionally, we and others have recently reported a set of transcription factors (TFs) associated with the expression of color and the emission of fragrance in <i>O. fragrans</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Baldermann, S. et al. Functional characterization of a carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase 1 and its relation to the carotenoid accumulation and volatile emission during the floral development of Osmanthus fragrans Lour. J. Exp. Bot. 61, 2967â2977 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d50438e705">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Mu, H. N. et al. Transcriptome sequencing and analysis of sweet osmanthus (Osmanthus fragrans Lour.). Genes. Genom. 36, 777â788 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d50438e708">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Han, Y. J. et al. Characterization of OfWRKY3, a transcription factor that positively regulates the carotenoid cleavage dioxygenase gene OfCCD4 in Osmanthus fragrans. Plant Mol. Biol. 91, 485â496 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d50438e711">27</a></sup>. All of these gene expression studies provide valuable insights on how flower blooming and aroma production are interlinked<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wang, L. et al. Analysis of the main active ingredients and bioactivities of essential oil from Osmanthus fragrans Var. thunbergii using a complex network approach. BMC Syst. Biol. 11, 144 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d50438e715">28</a></sup>. However, a genome sequence is largely needed to reveal the full genetic background of aroma production in sweet osmanthus and the evolution of aroma in the family Oleaceae.</p><p>In this study, we generated a reference genome for <i>O. fragrans</i> to provide a solid foundation for our future understanding of the genome structure and evolution of the Oleaceae family. Furthermore, we conducted a detailed analysis of the aroma compounds, tissue and flowering time-specific differential gene expression to investigate the molecular mechanisms of sweet fragrance development in <i>O. fragrans</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing summary</h3><p>We generated 100-fold PacBio single-molecule long reads (a total of 73.4âGb with an N50 length of 13.0âkb), 77-fold <i>k</i>-mer depth Illumina paired-end short reads (45.1âGb) and Hi-C data that produced 23 unambiguous chromosome scaffolds for a high-quality assembly. For stepwise assembly, we first performed an initial PacBio-only assembly, resulting in an assembly size of 733.5âMb and a contig N50 of 1.59âMb, and the assembled genome had a highly complete BUSCOs (96.1 %) (Supplemental TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#MOESM1">1</a>). Then, the initial contigs were subsequently polished with PacBio long reads and Illumina short reads. As the final step, Hi-C data were used to polish the scaffolds generated by the PacBio and Illumina reads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Determination of genome size and heterozygosity</h3><p>The <i>k</i>-mer method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Guillaume, M. &amp; Carl, K. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of K-Mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764â770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d50438e757">29</a></sup> and KmerFreqAR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 27, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d50438e761">30</a></sup> were used to determine the genome size of <i>O. fragrans</i> using the quality-filtered reads of Illumina data. The genome size was estimated based on the formula: Genome sizeâ=âModified <i>k</i>-mer number/average <i>k</i>-mer depth, where modified <i>k</i>-merâ=âTotal <i>k</i>-mer numberâerror <i>k</i>-mer number and the average <i>k</i>-mer depth obtained from the main peak of <i>k</i>-mer distribution curve (Supplemental Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#MOESM1">1</a>). To determine the heterozygosity, <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome data were used to simulate Illumina PE reads, which was carried out by using pIRS software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Hu, X. et al. pIRS: Profile-based Illumina pair-end reads simulator. Bioinformatics 28, 1533â1535 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d50438e797">31</a></sup>. Then, a fitting KmerFreqAR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 27, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d50438e801">30</a></sup> was developed using the <i>k</i>-mer distribution curve of <i>O. fragrans</i>. When the two <i>k</i>-mer curves were consistent, the heterozygosity of <i>Arabidopsis</i> was considered the reference for the heterozygosity of <i>O. fragrans</i>. The final analysis produced ~1.45% heterozygosity of the <i>O. fragrans</i> genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome assembly and quality assessment</h3><p>The integrated work-flow of genome assembly is shown (Supplemental Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#MOESM1">2</a>). The full PacBio long reads were converted to fasta format. Then, all subreads of genome data were assembled using Falcon v0.3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050â1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d50438e835">32</a></sup> with specific parameters (length-cutoff prâ=â8âkb; length-cutoff prâ=â9âkb). We used Arrow (<a href="https://github.com">https://github.com</a>) to polish the draft genome (G1) to obtain the corrected genome (G2). Then, G2 was polished again by Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Walker, B. J., Abeel, T., Shea, T., Priest, M., Abouelliel, A., Sakthikumar, S., Cuomo, C. A., Zeng, Q., Wortman, J., Young, S. K. &amp; Earl, A. M. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d50438e846">33</a></sup>, which mapped the next-generation sequencing data to G2 with bwa to obtain the twice-corrected genome (G3). The <i>O. fragrans</i> genome had high heterozygosity, which led to a G3 size larger than the estimation. To acquire the nonredundant genome, heterozygous and redundant sequences were removed from the corrected genome using Redundans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Pryszcz, L. P. &amp; GabaldÃ³n, T. Redundans: an assembly pipeline for highly heterozygous genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, e113 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d50438e854">34</a></sup> with the following parameters: heterozygosityâ=â0.0145 and Sequencing Depthâ=â86. The nonredundant genome (G4) was ~741âMb, with a contig N50 size of 1.595âMb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Tab1">1</a>). Finally, BUSCO v3.0 analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with Single-Copy Orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d50438e861">35</a></sup> was performed to assess G4 using the embryophyta_odb10 database with default parameters.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Quality assessment statistics of the assembled genome of <i>O. fragrans</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The clustering of contig by hierarchical clustering of the Hi-C data was performed. Through a comparative analysis, the only pair of reads around the DpnII digestion site was determined. Hi-C linkage was used as a criterion to measure the degree of tightness of the association between different contigs by standardizing the digestion sites of DpnII on the genome sketch. Agglomerative hierarchical clustering and LACHESIS produced chromosome assembly maps with a karyotype of 2<i>n</i>â=â46 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig1">1</a>). As a result, the total number of contigs of the <i>O. fragrans</i> genome map was 5327, and the total length was 740,635,307âbp. The combined length of Hi-C contigs was 740,404,543âbp, accounted for 99.97Â % of the total length of the final assembled genome, indicating the high quality of Hi-C data (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Tab2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Annotation of repeat sequences</h3><p>The genome of <i>O. fragrans</i> had simply, moderately, and highly repetitive sequences. MIcroSAtellite was used to identify the repeat sequences in the genome of <i>O. fragrans</i> (MISA, RRID: SCR 010765). A total of 409,691 SSRs was obtained, including 305,868 mono-, 70,587 di-, 25,544 tri-, 3934 tetra-, 2081 penta-, and 1991 hexa-nucleotide repeats, respectively (Supplemental TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#MOESM1">2</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#MOESM1">3</a>). The tandem repeats finder (TRF, v4.07b)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, W309âW312 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1149">36</a></sup> identified over 400,000 tandem repeats, accounting for 0.076Â % of the <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> genome.</p><p>We used homology-based and de novo approaches to identify transposable elements. RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinforma. 4, 10 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1163">37</a></sup> was used to search against the Repbase (v. 22.11)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K. K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase Update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mob. DNA 6, 11 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1167">38</a></sup> and Mips-REdat libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Nussbaumer, T. et al. MIPS PlantsDB: a database framework for comparative plant genome research. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, D1144âD1151 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1171">39</a></sup>. Then, we used RepeatMasker v4.0.6 to search the de novo repeat library that we built using RepeatModeler v1.0.11 (RepeatModeler, RRID: SCR 015027). Finally, TEs were confirmed by searching the TE protein database using a RepeatProteinMask and WU-BLASTX. The repetitive sequence was 49.35Â %, of which LTR accounted for 28.49Â % of the assembled genome of <i>O. fragrans</i> (Supplemental TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Annotation of noncoding RNA (ncRNA)</h3><p>We identified rRNA, miRNA, and snRNA genes in the <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> genome by searching the Rfam database (release 13.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Kalvari, I. et al. Rfam 13.0: shifting to a genome-centric resource for non-coding RNA families. Nucleic Acids Res. 46, D335âD342 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1196">40</a></sup>, using BLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and tapplications. BMC Bioinforma. 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1200">41</a></sup> (<i>E</i>-valueââ¤â1eâ5). Software tRNAscan-SE (v1.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1208">42</a></sup> and RNAmmer v1.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Lagesen, K., Hallin, P., RÃ¸dland, E. A., Staerfeldt, H. H. &amp; Rognes, T. RNAmmer: consistent and rapid annotation of ribosomal RNA genes. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, 3100â3108 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1212">43</a></sup> were used to predict tRNAs and rRNAs, resulting in an <i>O</i>. <i>frangrans</i> genome with 525 miRNAs, 847 tRNAs, 49 rRNAs, and 2058 snRNAs (Supplemental TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Gene prediction</h3><p>The protein-coding genes were identified using homology-based and de novo predictions-based approaches. The <i>O. fragrans</i> genome was mapped against the published sequences of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Olea europaea</i>, <i>Sesamum indicum</i>, <i>Solanum tuberosum</i>, and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>. To accurately identify spliced alignments, we used GeneWise v2.2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Birney, E. &amp; Durbin, R. Using GeneWise in the Drosophila annotation experiment. Genome Res. 10, 547â548 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1252">44</a></sup> to filter all initially aligned coding sequences. For de novo prediction, the data from NGS and the full-length transcriptomes were analyzed with hisat2-2.1.0 and PASApipeline-2.0.2 to predict the complete gene set. We randomly selected 1000 genes to train the model parameters for Augustus v3.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, W309âW312 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1256">36</a></sup>, GeneID v1.4.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Blanco, E., Parra, G. &amp; GuigÃ³, R. Using geneid to identify genes. Curr. Protoc. Bioinforma. 4, 1â28 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1260">45</a></sup>, GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1264">46</a></sup>, and SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Bromberg, Y. &amp; Rost, B. SNAP: predict effect of non-synonymous polymorphisms on function. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, 3823â3835 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1268">47</a></sup>. The final consensus gene set was generatedusing EVidenceModeler (EVM) v1.1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1273">48</a></sup>, which combined the genes predicted by the de novo and homology searches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Moriya, Y., Itoh, M., Okuda, S., Yoshizawa, A. C. &amp; Kanehisa, M. KAAS: an automatic genome annotation and pathway reconstruction server. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W182âW185 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1277">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 116â120 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1280">50</a></sup> The assembled genome had 45,542 genes with an average transcript length of 4065âbp, an average CDS length of 1142âbp, and a number of exons per gene of 5 (Supplemental TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><p>The functional validity of the predicted genes was further evaluated by searching the UniProt (release 2017_10), KEGG (release 84.0), and InterPro (5.21-60.0) databases using Blastall44, KAAS,49, and InterProScan50. As a result, we were able to assign potential functions to 43,573 protein-coding genes out of the total of 45,542 genes in the <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> genome (95.68Â %) (Supplemental TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genome evolution</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec10">Gene family analysis</h4><p>Although morphological investigation and a number of genes have placed <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> in the Oleaceae family, there is still no whole genome-scale phylogenomic analysis of the evolutionary position of <i>O. fragrans</i>. Here, we compared the <i>O. fragrans</i> genome with the genome sequences of 11 other plants (<i>A. thaliana, Fraxinus excelsior, Glycine max, O. europaea, Oryza sativa, Petunia axillaris, Petunia inflata, Prunus mume, Rosa chinensis, Solanum lycopersicum</i>, and <i>V</i>. <i>vinifera)</i>. We applied the OrthoMCL (v2.0.9) pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="De Bodt, S., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Genome duplication and the origin of angiosperms. Trends Ecol. Evol. 20, 591â597 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d50438e1333">51</a></sup> (BLASTP <i>E</i>-valueââ¤â1eâ5) to identify the potential orthologous gene families between the genomes of these plants. Gene family clustering identified 17,513 gene families consisting of 38,808 genes in <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i>, of which, 1086 gene families were unique to <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i>. <i>O</i>. <i>europaea</i>, and <i>F</i>. <i>excelsior</i> had the biggest number of shared gene families among these plants (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Species tree and evolution of gene numbers."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Species tree and evolution of gene numbers.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41438_2018_108_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41438_2018_108_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="426" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> The phylogenetic tree showing the close relationship between sweet osmanthus and the wild olive (<i>O</i>. <i>europaea</i>). <b>b</b> The number of genes in various plant species, showing the high gene number of <i>O. fragrans</i> compared to a model (<i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i>) and other tree species. The number of multiple copy paralogs is high in <i>O. fragrans</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec11">Synteny analysis</h4><p>We used the protein sequences of <i>O. fragrans</i> that were aligned against each other with Blastp (<i>E</i>-valueââ¤â1eâ5) to achieve the conserved paralogs, Then, MCScanX (<a href="http://chibba.pgml.uga.edu/mcscab2">http://chibba.pgml.uga.edu/mcscab2</a>) was used to find the collinearity block in the genome. Using the Circos tool (<a href="http://www.circos.ca">http://www.circos.ca</a>), we mapped and gene density, GC content, Gypsy density, and Copia density, as well as the average expression value of genes expressed in flowers on individual chromosomes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="High-quality assembly of twenty-three chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: High-quality assembly of twenty-three chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41438_2018_108_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41438_2018_108_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="721" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Gene density, <b>b</b> GC content, <b>c</b> Gypsy density, <b>d</b> Copia density, and <b>e</b> average expression values of genes specifically expressed in flowers (from outside to inside)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec12">Whole-genome duplication (WGD)</h4><p>To determine the source of the high number of genes (&gt;45,000) in <i>O. fragrans</i>, the WGD events were analyzed by taking advantage of the high-quality genome of <i>O. fragrans</i>. We applied four-fold synonymous third-codon transversion (4DTv) and synonymous substitution rate (Ks) estimation to detect the WGD events. First, respective paralogous of <i>O. fragrans</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>O</i>. <i>europaea</i>, <i>V</i>. <i>vinifera</i>, and <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i> were identified with OrthoMCL. Then, the protein sequences of these plants were aligned against each other with Blastp (<i>E</i>-valueââ¤â1eâ5) to achieve the conserved paralogs of each plant. Finally, the WGD events of each plant were evaluated based on their 4DTv (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig4">4a</a>) or Ks (data not shown) distribution. The WGD analysis suggestted that <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i>, <i>G</i>. <i>max</i> and <i>O</i>. <i>europaea</i> experienced WGD events within less than 15 MYA, but <i>V</i>. <i>vinifer</i> and <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i> have not experienced WGD events recently (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig4">4a</a>). We also compared the number of duplicated genes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig4">4b</a>), the chromosome-level duplications (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig4">4c</a>), and the number of a functional homologs of glycotransferase and bHLH-Myc transcription factor genes between <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> and <i>V</i>. <i>vinifera</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig4">4d</a>), further validating the WGD events.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Evidences for whole-genome duplication events in O. fragrans."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Evidences for whole-genome duplication events in O. fragrans.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41438_2018_108_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41438_2018_108_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="670" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> 4DTV; <b>b</b> the most abundant genes (red bar: duplicated genes, green: non-duplicated, 0: tandem-duplicated or small-scale-duplicated genes; 0â12, number of duplicated copies) in a four-fold relationship when comparing <i>O. fragrans</i> and grapes. <b>c</b> A colinear relationship at the chromosome level. <b>d</b> Four-fold expansion of the functional homologs of glycosyltransferase and bHLH-MYC genes in grape (GSVIVT) and <i>O. frgrans</i> (evm.model.Contig). The data suggest that <i>O. fragrans</i> experienced two WGD events</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Determination of volatile aroma compounds</h3><p>To make a direct connection between the biosynthetic genes and flower fragrance development, we determined the volatile aroma compounds. Headspace-SPME combined with GC-MS analysis identified over 40 volatile compounds, including linalool, dihydrojasmone lactone (2(3H)-furanone, 5-hexyldihydro-), 1-cyclohexene-1-propanol, 2,6,6-tetramethyl-, and <i>Î²</i>-ocimen as the major components. Linalool was present in the highest amount at the early flowering stage (S1) and decreased afterwards (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Tab3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Identity and quantity of volatile aroma compounds in the various flowering stages of <i>O. fragrans</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Expression analysis</h3><p>We also produced comprehensive transcriptome dataset using both HiSeq and the Iso-Seq pipeline. We focused our further analysis on identifying the specific genes responsible for floral development and the biosynthesis of volatile aroma compounds in <i>O. fragrans</i>. The members of MADs transcription factors that control plant development were highly expressed in all tissues tested. Among them, AG, AP3/PI, AP1, and SEP were predominantly expressed in the early flower stage (S1), whereas, the expression level of the <i>ANR1</i> gene family was highly specific to the root tissue (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig5">5b</a>). Interestingly, the numbers of ABCE genes were higher than that of <i>Fraxinus chinensis</i>, a close relative of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig5">5a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="MADS-box gene family in O. fragrans."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: MADS-box gene family in O. fragrans.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/figures/5"
