/></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> The evolution tree and expression values of the MADSs box genes, <b>b</b> Heatmap showing the tissue- and flowering stage- specific expressions of the members of ABCE genes</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The major component of the volatile compounds in the floral scent of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i>, linalool (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig6">6</a>), is known to be synthesized by terpenoid synthetases (TPS). Therefore, we compared the expression profiles of <i>TPS</i> genes and identified over 40 genes that contain the functional motifs of TPS. Differential gene expression (DGE) analysis identified 7 <i>TPS</i> genes that are highly expressed in flowers, compared to roots, leaves, and stems (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig7">7</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="The top 7 secondary metabolites produced by the osmanthus flower measured by GC-MS."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: The top 7 secondary metabolites produced by the osmanthus flower measured by GC-MS.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41438_2018_108_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41438_2018_108_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="442" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Note that the top molecule is linalool, a monoterpene, which was produced most in the S1 stage of the flower, and then decreased its amount in the flower. S1, S2, and S3 stand for the early, middle, and late stages, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="TPS (terpene synthase) gene family in O. fragrans."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7: TPS (terpene synthase) gene family in O. fragrans.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41438_2018_108_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-018-0108-0/MediaObjects/41438_2018_108_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1082" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p><b>a</b> Heatmap showing the tissue- and flowering stage-specific expression of the members of TPS genes, <b>b</b> TPS gene subfamily members. Yellow: S1 early flowering stage-specific Red: S3, late flowering stage-specific</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><p>Sweet osmanthus is one of the most beloved ornamental tree species in China and other parts of the world and has been cultivated for over two-thousand years in China due to its attractive traits of beautiful colors, unique aromas, a long flowering season, and medicinal efficacy. However, there is a limited number of studies that have investigated the genetic basis of the phenotypic diversity of sweet osmanthus. Recently, a set of genetic markers was identified<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Yuan, W. J., Li, Y., Ma, Y. F., Han, Y. J. &amp; Shang, F. D. Isolation and characterization of microsatellite markers for Osmanthus fragrans (Oleaceae) using 454 sequencing technology. Genet. Mol. Res. 14, 17154â17158 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3381">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Han, Y. J. et al. cDNA-AFLP analysis on 2 Osmanthus fragrans cultivars with different flower color and molecular characteristics of MYB1gene. Trees 29, 931â940 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3384">15</a></sup>, and an effort to construct a genetic linkage map was reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="He, Y. X., Yuan, W. J., Dong, M. F., Han, Y. J. &amp; Shang, F. D. The first genetic map in sweet osmanthus (Osmanthus fragrans Lour.) using specific locus amplified fragment sequencing. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 1621 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3388">16</a></sup>. Additionally, several transcriptomics studies identified a large number of genes that are differentially expressed in some of the cultivars with attractive traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zhang, X. S., Pei, J. J., Zhao, L. G., Tang, F. &amp; Fang, X. Y. RNA-Seq analysis and comparison of the enzymes involved in ionone synthesis of three cultivars of Osmanthus. J. Asian Nat. Prod. Res. 9, 1â13 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3392">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yang, X. L. et al. Transcriptomic analysis of the candidate genes related to aroma formation in Osmanthus fragrans. Molecules 23, 1604 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3395">18</a></sup>. While these studies indirectly associate the diverse phenotypes with the genotypes of sweet osmanthus, there is no genome information that can directly link the specific genes to particular traits. Thus, we have sequenced, assembled and annotated the genome of sweet osmanthus. Furthermore, combining HiSeq- and IsoSeq-based transcriptome analyses, we gained deep insight into the genes that control aroma compounds synthesis in the flowers of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">The high-quality reference genome provides deep insights to the evolution of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i>
                        </h3><p>Currently, there are still no comprehensive analyses combining genomic, transcriptomic, and metabolic approaches to reveal the unique aroma of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i>. Despite advances in second-generation sequencing, it is still very challenging to construct a high-quality plant genome due to the high complexity, large size, and high percentage of repeats and polyploidy. Therefore, we combined the second-generation short read to achieve high accuracy, the third-generation long reads for de novo assembly, and Hi-C to scaffold contigs into a chromosome-scale assembly. To guarantee a high-quality genome annotation, we combined <i>de novo</i>, homology-based, and experimental evidence obtained from the extensive transcriptomics data, including the full-length transcripts. We constructed a reference-quality genome that produced an unambiguous chromosome-scale assembly (<i>N</i>â=â23) and functionally annotated 43,573 genes out of the complete set of 45,542 genes of <i>O. fragrans</i> (95.68 %).</p><p>The number of genes, 45,542, is high and is more than the genes present in some of the plants that are related to <i>O. fragrans</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig2">2</a>). This can be attributed to the repeated gene duplications which led to expansion of the gene families. The <i>O. fragrans</i> genome has higher number of multicopy genes compared to other plant species (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig2">2</a>). Furthermore, <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> appears to have obtained and retained a large number of genes through the whole genome duplications (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig4">4</a>). The majority of plant species have experienced genome duplications in their evolutionary past<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3459">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Ciu, L. Y. et al. Widespread genome duplications throughout the history of flowering plants. Genome Res. 16, 738â749 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3462">53</a></sup>. The high gene number of <i>O. fragrans</i> might be a result of complex interactions among various factors such as the rate of evolution, number of duplication events, level of gene retention, expansion of gene family and selection pressure. The recent (~15 MYA) WGD and high retention might explain the large gene number. The number of genes involved in secondary metabolism is particularly high in <i>O. fragrans</i> (unpublished observation), and these genes might have been retained and/or expanded after the whole-genome duplications. This result may reflect the continuous interaction between <i>O. fragrans</i> and environmental factors, which imposes a constant pressure for adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Casneuf, T., De Bodt, S., Raes, J., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Nonrandom divergence of gene expression following gene and genome duplications in the flowering plants Arabidopsis thaliana. Genome Biol. 7, R13 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3476">54</a></sup>.</p><p>The calculated level of heterozygosity (1.45Â %) is high in <i>O. fragrans</i> var ruixiangui. Considering that <i>O. fragrans</i> has been selectively bred for desirable traits for over 2000 years in China, 160 cultivars with diverse phenotypes have been selected. The high heterozygosity (1.45Â %) in <i>O. fragrans</i> var ruixiangui might support an extensive breeding among cultivars throughout its history, although it is challenging to accurately determine the origin of the observed heterozygosity in the cultivar. Furthermore, as an androdioecious species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Xu, Y. C. et al. The differentiation and development of pistils of hermaphrodites and pistillodes of males in androdioecious Osmanthus fragrans L. and implications for the evolution to androdioecy. Plant Syst. Evol. 300, 843â849 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3492">55</a></sup>, the coexistence of selfing and crossing poses an additional challenge to trace the origin of the high heterozygosity. Recently, the first genetic map of <i>O. fragrans</i> was created using the SLAF-seq method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="He, Y. X., Yuan, W. J., Dong, M. F., Han, Y. J. &amp; Shang, F. D. The first genetic map in sweet osmanthus (Osmanthus fragrans Lour.) using specific locus amplified fragment sequencing. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 1621 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3500">16</a></sup> to provide a framework for understanding the genome organization. This linkage map has helped us assemble the reference genome and can help to investigate the origin of the high heterozygosity and history of hybridization among the cultivars of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i>.</p><p>The new genome can also be used as a reference for the whole genome resequencing of sweet osmanthus cultivars. Resequencing these whole genomes of various cultivars provides highly useful information on the potential drivers for the phenotype diversity, evolution, and population structure of a given species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Huang, X. et al. High-throughput genotyping by whole-genome resequencing. Genome Res. 19, 1068â1076 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3513">56</a></sup>. Our preliminary genome sequencing of 30 different cultivars of <i>O. fragrans</i> identified a large number of single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNP), copy number variation (CNV), insertion sdeletion (InDel), structural variations (SV) and other mutation sites (unpublished results). Using the above mutation loci as new molecular genetic markers, researchers can study the history of cultivation, population dynamics and genetic diversity.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">The whole-genome duplication and the tandem duplication of the biosynthetic genes is likely the cause for the strong sweet aroma of <i>O. fragrans</i>
                        </h3><p>Among <i>TPS</i>-family genes, the <i>TPS-b</i> and <i>g</i> subfamilies are known to synthesize monoterpenes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Tholl, D. Terpene synthases and the regulation, diversity and biological roles of terpene metabolism. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 9, 297â304 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3541">57</a></sup>. Linalool, the major aroma compound identified in our study, is produced by the monoterpene synthesis pathway in <i>O. fragrans</i>. Using the high-quality genome and deep transcriptome information, we found a significant expansion of TPS as a whole, and of subfamilies b and g specifically, compared with the grape (<i>V</i>. <i>vinifera</i>), which did not have whole genome duplication (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#Fig5">5</a>). In addition to TPS1, 2, 3, and 4, which have been previously functionally validated, we identified seven additional <i>TPS</i> genes that are specifically expressed in flower stages S1 and S3. Three <i>TPS</i> genes appear to be new genes that are flower specific, indicating that the production of fragrance is controlled by a complex network involving multiple <i>TPS</i> genes functioning in time- and flower-specific manners. Our results suggest that the unique aromas of <i>O. fragrans</i> are some of the outcomes of the interrelationship between genome evolution, transcriptional regulation, and metabolic control. Our current work lays a solid foundation for further studies on the comparative genomics, molecular and biochemical mechanisms of aroma development in <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec18" data-title="Conclusion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec18-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec18">Conclusion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec18-content"><p>We constructed a high-quality reference genome of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> by combining Illumina, PacBio and Hi-C platforms. The genome of <i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> var. rixianggui is ~740âMb and has a high heterozygosity of 1.45Â %. A large number of genes (45,542) was predicted by the gene models built with de novo, homology-based, and experimental data obtained from extensive transcription results. Our deep genome analysis indicates evidence of whole-genome duplication at ~15 MYA. Our new genome information should help the research community study the genome structure, genetic basis of genetic diversity, and regulation of the flowering process and scent development in <i>O. fragrans</i> and other related plant species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec19" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec19-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec19">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec19-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Genome sequencing</h3><p>For genome sequencing, leaf samples were collected from a male tree (<i>O</i>. <i>fragrans</i> var. rixianggui) on the campus of Nanjing Forestry University, Nanjing, China, and were processed for genomic DNA isolation and library construction. Rixianggui (Semperfloren) is a unique cultivar because it has a strong aroma and blooms continuously, except in hot summer months, while other cultivars, for example, Thunbergii, Latifolius, and Aurantiaeus, bloom only in autumn. Genomic DNA was extracted using the CTAB method, size fractionated with BluePipin (Sage Science, Inc, MA, USA), used for library construction following the PacBio SMRT library construction protocol, and sequenced on the PacBio Sequel platform (Pacific Biosciences, CA, USA). For Illumina library construction, the extracted DNA was fragmented and size-fractionated using g-tube and BluePipin, then subjected to paired-end library construction and sequenced on the HiSeq X ten platform (Illumina Inc, CA, USA).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Hi-C sequencing</h3><p>To ensure the quality of the Hi-C library, leaf samples were initially examined forintegrity of the nuclei by DAPI staining. Once confirmed for high quality nuclei, the samples were processed following the Hi-C procedure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kaplan, N. &amp; Dekker, J. High-throughput genome scaffolding from in vivo DNA interaction frequency. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1143â1147 (2013)." href="#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3627">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Marie-Nelly, H. et al. High-quality genome (re) assembly using chromosomal contact data. Nat. Commun. 5, 5695 (2014)." href="#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3627_1">59</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Jibran, R. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of the black raspberry (Rubus occidentalis L.) genome based on chromatin interaction data. Hortic. Res. 5, 18008 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3630">60</a></sup>. The Hi-C library was sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq X ten platform (Illumina, CA, USA), generating 740 million Hi-C read pairs, which were submitted to the Lachesis Hi-C scaffolding pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Kaplan, N. &amp; Dekker, J. High-throughput genome scaffolding from in vivo DNA interaction frequency. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1143â1147 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d50438e3634">58</a></sup>. Hi-C libraries produce different molecular types, including invalid pairs of self-circles, dangling-ends, and dumped-pairs. According to the different molecular types that lead to the alignment of paired reads on the genome in different directions, the unique alignment of reads on the genome needs to be statistically analyzed. Once recognized as an effective interaction, the final data only retained effective interactions. According to the above rules, the position of the DpnII digestion site in the reference genome was used, because it can also provide useful information on the structural organization of individual chromosomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>To obtain information that can assist in the empirical annotation of genes, full-length transcriptome sequencing was performed. The samples from flowers at three different blooming stages (S1: beginning, S2: middle, S3: late; Suppl. FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#MOESM1">3</a>), leaves, stems, and roots were collected from the same tree described above and processed for library construction. The total RNAs were extracted according to the manufacturerâs instructions of TRNzol Universal Reagent (Cat# DP424, TIANGEN Biotech Co. Ltd, Beijing, China). The quality and quantity of the RNA samples were evaluated using a NanoDropâ¢ One UV-Vis spectrophotometer (Thermo Fisher Scientific, USA), a QubitÂ® 3.0 Fluorometer (Thermo Fisher Scientific, USA) and an Agilent Bioanalyzer 2100 (Agilent Technologies, USA). All RNA samples with integrity values close to 10 were used for cDNA library construction and sequencing. The cDNA library was prepared using the TruSeq Sample Preparation (Illumina Inc, CA, USA) and IsoSeq Library Construction kits (Pacific Biosciences, CA, USA), and paired-end sequencing with 150âbp was conducted on a HiSeq X ten platform (Illumina Inc, CA, USA).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec23">Aroma compound analysis</h3><p>Fresh flowers at three different stages (S1: beginning, S2: middle, S3: lat), defined by the size of the flower (Supplemental Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-018-0108-0#MOESM1">3</a>), were picked from the same tree at the time of sample collection for the transcriptome studies described above. Sampling was replicated five times, and the samples were quickly put into polyethylene bags impermeable to gases, kept frozen and stored at â20 Â°C. Headspace solid phase microextraction (SPME) combined with gas chromatography-mass spectrometry (GC-MS) was used to determine the identity and quantity of the aroma volatiles. Flowers (0.3âg) were placed in a 4âmL solid-phase microextraction vial (Supelco Inc, USA), 1âÎ¼l of 1000Ã diluted ethyl caprate (Macklin Inc, China) was added, and vials were capped with a 65âÂµm DB-5 ms extraction head (Supelco Inc, USA). Then, the vial was incubated for 40âmin in a water bath at 45Â Â°C to volatilize the aroma compounds and release them into the headspace. After the adsorption period, the fiber head was removed and introduced into the heated injector port of the GC for desorption at 250Â Â°C for 3âmin. The desorbed volatile compounds from DB-5 ms were analyzed on a Trace DSQ GC-MS (Thermo-Fisher Scientific, USA), equipped with a 30âm x 0.25âmmâÃâ0.25âmm TR-5 ms capillary column (Supelco Inc, USA). The oven temperature was programmed at 60Â Â°C for 2âmin, increasing at 5Â Â°C/min to 150Â Â°C, then increasing at 10Â Â°C/min to reach 250Â Â°C, followed by maintaining the temperature of the transfer line at 250Â Â°C. Helium was taken as the carrier gas at a linear velocity of 1.0âmL/min. Mass detector conditions on MS were: source temperature: 250Â Â°C and the electronic impact (EI) mode at 70âeV, with a speed of 4 scans/s over the mass range m/z 33-450âamu in a 1âs cycle. Volatile compounds were first auto-matched by mass spectra using the NIST98 database through ChemStation (Agilent, USA). A series of <i>n</i>-alkanes (C7-C30) (Sigma St. Louis, MO) was injected into the GC-MS set to obtain the linear retention indices of the volatile compounds, and they were analyzed under the same conditions. The data were also compared with published linear retention indices (NIST Chemistry WebBook, SRD 69). The normalization of peak-areas was used to calculate the quantities of the volatile aroma compounds.</p></div></div></section>

                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Zhen Huang</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Wenfang Fan</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Jiannan Chen</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Wan Cen</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Shuai Chen</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Jinmao Zhu</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Binghua Chen</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Xingtan Zhang</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">&nbsp;&amp;&nbsp;</span><span>Youqiang Chen</span></li></ul>
              
              <p class="c-article-further-reading__journal-title" id="journal-name-5">
                <i>Horticulture Research</i>
                (2020)
              </p>
            </li>
          
        </ul>
      </div>
    </div>
    </section>

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">Genome sequences: Discovering a sweet secret</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>A high-quality genome sequence for sweet olive, <i>Osmanthus fragrans</i>, has revealed which genes make the ornamental treeâs blossoms so fragrant. Lianggui Wang at Nanjing Forestry University in China and coworkers used cutting-edge approaches to produce a high-resolution picture of sweet oliveâs genome. Analysis of the full sequence revealed that sweet olive has a large number of genes, more than 45,000. Further investigation showed that the entire genome had been duplicated, or accidentally copied, several million years ago. Thanks to the duplication, sweet olive has extra copies of the genes that produce its scent compound, resulting in its very strong scent. These results are only the beginning. Having this first sequence makes sequencing other varieties of sweet olive much easier, and future comparisons between genomes will help to pinpoint genes that control many other traits.</p></div></section></div></aside>
